10100-8{_}1930{_}to prefer the claw of the Tartar savage to the paternal hug of the great Bear--    Better still toil for masters, than await,   The slave of slaves, before a Russian gate.
10100-8{_}1931{_}In the _Island_--a tale of the mutiny of the "Bounty"--he reverts to the manner and theme of his old romances, finding a new scene in the Pacific for the exercise of his fancy.
10100-8{_}1932{_}In this piece his love of nautical adventure reappears, and his idealization of primitive life, caught from Rousseau and Chateaubriand.
10100-8{_}1933{_}There is more repose about this poem than in any of the author's other compositions.
10100-8{_}1934{_}In its pages the sea seems to plash about rocks and caves that bask under a southern sun.
10100-8{_}1935{_}"'Byron, the sorcerer,' he can do with me what he will," said old Dr. Parr, on reading it.
10100-8{_}1936{_}As the swan-song of the poet's sentimental verse, it has a pleasing if not pathetic calm.
10100-8{_}1937{_}During the last years in Italy he planned an epic on the Conquest, and a play on the subject of Hannibal, neither of which was executed.
10100-8{_}1938{_}In the criticism of a famous work there is often little left to do but to criticise the critics--to bring to a focus the most salient things that have been said about it, to eliminate the absurd from the sensible, the discriminating from the commonplace.
10100-8{_}1939{_}_Don Juan_, more than any of its precursors, _is_ Byron, and it has been similarly handled.
10100-8{_}1940{_}The early cantos were ushered into the world amid a chorus of mingled applause and execration.
10100-8{_}1941{_}The minor Reviews, representing middle-class respectability, were generally vituperative, and the higher authorities divided in their judgments.
10100-8{_}1942{_}The _British Magazine_ said that "his lordship had degraded his personal character by the composition;" the _London_, that the poem was "a satire on decency;" the _Edinburgh Monthly_, that it was "a melancholy spectacle;" the _Eclectic_, that it was "an outrage worthy of detestation."
10100-8{_}1943{_}_Blackwood_ declared that the author was "brutally outraging all the best feelings of humanity."
10100-8{_}1944{_}Moore characterizes it as "the most painful display of the versatility of genius that has ever been left for succeeding ages to wonder at or deplore."
10100-8{_}1945{_}Jeffrey found in the whole composition "a tendency to destroy all belief in the reality of virtue;" and Dr. John Watkins classically named it "the Odyssey of Immorality."
10100-8{_}1946{_}"_Don Juan_ will be read," wrote one critic, "as long as satire, wit, mirth, and supreme excellence shall be esteemed among men."
10100-8{_}1947{_}"Stick to _Don Juan_," exhorted another; "it is the only sincere thing you have written, and it will live after all your _Harolds_ have ceased to be 'a schoolgirl's tale, the wonder of an hour.'
10100-8{_}1948{_}It is the best of all your works--the most spirited, the most straightforward, the most interesting, the most poetical."
10100-8{_}1949{_}"It is a work," said Goethe, "full of soul, bitterly savage in its misanthropy, exquisitely delicate in its tenderness."
10100-8{_}1950{_}Shelley confessed, "It fulfils in a certain degree what I have long preached, the task of producing something wholly new and relative to the age, and yet surpassingly beautiful."
10100-8{_}1951{_}And Sir Walter Scott, in the midst of a hearty panegyric: "It has the variety of Shakespeare himself.
10100-8{_}1952{_}Neither _Childe Harold_, nor the most beautiful of Byron's earlier tales, contain more exquisite poetry than is to be found scattered through the cantos of _Don Juan_, amidst verses which the author seems to have thrown from him with an effort as spontaneous as that of a tree resigning its leaves."
10100-8{_}1953{_}One noticeable feature about these comments is their sincerity: reviewing, however occasionally one-sided, had not then sunk to be the mere register of adverse or friendly cliques; and, with all his anxiety for its verdict, Byron never solicited the favour of any portion of the press.
10100-8{_}1954{_}Another is, the fact that the adverse critics missed their mark.
10100-8{_}1955{_}They had not learnt to say of a book of which they disapproved, that it was weak or dull: in pronouncing it to be vicious, they helped to promote its sale; and the most decried has been the most widely read of the author's works.
10100-8{_}1956{_}Many of the readers of _Don Juan_ have, it must be confessed, been found among those least likely to admire in it what is most admirable--who have been attracted by the very excesses of buffoonery, violations of good taste, and occasionally almost vulgar slang, which disfigure its pages.
10100-8{_}1957{_}Their patronage is, at the best, of no more value than that of a mob gathered by a showy Shakespearian revival, and it has laid the volume open to the charge of being adapted "laudari ab illaudatis."
10100-8{_}1958{_}But the welcome of the work in other quarters is as indubitably duo to higher qualities.
10100-8{_}1959{_}In writing _Don Juan_, Byron attempted something that had never been done before, and his genius so chimed with his enterprise that it need never be done again.
10100-8{_}1960{_}"Down," cries M. Chasles, "with the imitators who did their host to make his name ridiculous."
10100-8{_}1961{_}In commenting on their failure, an Athenaeum critic has explained the pre-established fitness of the ottava rima--the first six lines of which are a dance, and the concluding couplet a "breakdown"--for the mock-heroic.
10100-8{_}1962{_}Byron's choice of this measure may have been suggested by Whistlecraft; but, he had studied its cadence in Pulci, and the _Novelle Galanti_ of Casti, to whom he is indebted for other features of his satire; and he added to what has been well termed its characteristic jauntiness, by his almost constant use of the double rhyme.
10100-8{_}1963{_}That the ottava rima is out of place in consistently pathetic poetry, may be seen from its obvious misuse in Keats's _Pot of Basil_.
10100-8{_}1964{_}Many writers, from Tennant and Frere to Moultrie, have employed it in burlesque or more society verse; but Byron alone has employed it triumphantly, for he has made it the vehicle of thoughts grave as well as gay, of "black spirits and white, red spirits and grey," of sparkling fancy, bitter sarcasm, and tender memories.
10100-8{_}1965{_}He has swept into the pages of his poem the experience of thirty years of a life so crowded with vitality that our sense of the plethora of power which it exhibits makes us ready to condone its lapses.
10100-8{_}1966{_}Byron, it has been said, balances himself on a ladder like other acrobats; but alone, like the Japanese master of the art, he all the while bears on his shoulders the weight of a man.
10100-8{_}1967{_}Much of _Don Juan_ is as obnoxious to criticism in detail as his earlier work; it has every mark of being written in hot haste.
10100-8{_}1968{_}In the midst of the most serious passages (e.g.
10100-8{_}1969{_}the "Ave Maria") we are checked in our course by bathos or commonplace and thrown where the writer did not mean to throw us: but the mocking spirit is so prevailingly present that we are often left in doubt as to his design, and what is in _Harold_ an outrage is in this case only a flaw.
10100-8{_}1970{_}His command over the verse itself is almost miraculous: he glides from extreme to extreme, from punning to pathos, from melancholy to mad merriment, sighing or laughing by the way at his readers or at himself or at the stanzas.
10100-8{_}1971{_}Into them he can fling anything under the sun, from a doctor's prescription to a metaphysical theory.
10100-8{_}1972{_}When Bishop Berkeley said there was no matter,   And proved it, 'twas no matter what he said,  is as cogent a refutation of idealism as the cumbrous wit of Scotch logicians.
10100-8{_}1973{_}The popularity of the work is due not mainly to the verbal skill which makes it rank as the _cleverest_ of English verse compositions, to its shoals of witticisms, its winged words, telling phrases, and incomparable transitions; but to the fact that it continues to address a large class who are not in the ordinary sense of the word lovers of poetry.
10100-8{_}1974{_}_Don Juan_ is emphatically the poem of intelligent men of middle age, who have grown weary of mere sentiment, and yet retain enough of sympathetic feeling to desire at times to recall it.
10100-8{_}1975{_}Such minds, crusted like Plato's Glaucus with the world, are yet pervious to appeals to the spirit that survives beneath the dry dust amid which they move; but only at rare intervals can they accompany the pure lyrist "singing as if he would never be old," and they are apt to turn with some impatience even from _Romeo and Juliet_ to _Hamlet_ and _Macbeth_.
10100-8{_}1976{_}To them, on the other hand, the hard wit of _Hudibras_ is equally tiresome, and more distasteful; their chosen friend is the humourist who, inspired by a subtle perception of the contradictions of life, sees matter for smiles in sorrow, and tears in laughter.
10100-8{_}1977{_}Byron was not, in the highest sense, a great humourist; he does not blend together the two phases, as they are blended in single sentences or whole chapters of Sterne, in the April-sunshine of Richter, or in _Sartor Resartus_; but he comes near to produce the same effect by his unequalled power of alternating them.
10100-8{_}1978{_}His wit is seldom hard, never dry, for it is moistened by the constant juxtaposition of sentiment.
10100-8{_}1979{_}His tenderness is none the less genuine that he is perpetually jerking it away--an equally favourite fashion with Carlyle,--as if he could not trust himself to be serious for fear of becoming sentimental; and, in recollection of his frequent exhibitions of unaffected hysteria, we accept his own confession--                 If I laugh at any mortal thing,                'Tis that I may not weep,  as a perfectly sincere comment on the most sincere, and therefore in many respects the most effective, of his works.
10100-8{_}1980{_}He has, after his way, endeavoured in grave prose and light verse to defend it against its assailants; saying, "In _Don Juan_ I take a vicious and unprincipled character, and lead him through those ranks of society whose accomplishments cover and cloak their vices, and paint the natural effects;" and elsewhere, that he means to make his scamp "end as a member of the Society for the Suppression of Vice, or by the guillotine, or in an unhappy marriage."
10100-8{_}1981{_}It were easy to dilate on the fact that in interpreting the phrases of the satirist into the language of the moralist we often require to read them backwards: Byron's own statement, "I hate a motive," is, however, more to the point:    But the fact is that I have nothing plann'd,   Unless it were to be a moment merry--   A novel word in my vocabulary.
10100-8{_}1982{_}_Don Juan_ can only be credited with a text in the sense in which every large experience, of its own accord, conveys its lesson.
10100-8{_}1983{_}It was to the author a picture of the world as he saw it; and it is to us a mirror in which every attribute of his genius, every peculiarity of his nature, is reflected without distortion.
10100-8{_}1984{_}After the audacious though brilliant opening, and the unfortunately pungent reference to the poet's domestic affairs, we find in the famous storm (c.
10100-8{_}1985{_}ii.)
10100-8{_}1986{_}a bewildering epitome of his prevailing manner.
10100-8{_}1987{_}Home-sickness, sea-sickness, the terror of the tempest, "wailing, blasphemy, devotion," the crash of the wreck, the wild farewell, "the bubbling cry of some strong swimmer in his agony," the horrors of famine, the tale of the two fathers, the beautiful apparitions of the rainbow and the bird, the feast on Juan's spaniel, his reluctance to dine on "his pastor and his master," the consequences of eating Pedrillo,--all follow each other like visions in the phantasmagoria of a nightmare, till at last the remnant of the crew are drowned by a ridiculous rhyme--    Finding no place for their landing better,   They ran the boat ashore,--and overset her.
10100-8{_}1988{_}Then comes the episode of Haidee, "a long low island song of ancient days," the character of the girl herself being like a thread of pure gold running through the fabric of its surroundings, motley in every page; e.g., after the impassioned close of the "Isles of Greece," we have the stanza:--    Thus sang, or would, or could, or should, have sung,     The modern Greek, in tolerable verse;   If not like Orpheus quite, when Greece was young,     Yet in those days he might have done much worse--  with which the author dashes away the romance of the song, and then launches into a tirade against Bob Southey's epic and Wordsworth's pedlar poems.
10100-8{_}1989{_}This vein exhausted, we come to the "Ave Maria," one of the most musical, and seemingly heartfelt, hymns in the language.
10100-8{_}1990{_}The close of the ocean pastoral (in c.
10100-8{_}1991{_}iv.)
10100-8{_}1992{_}is the last of pathetic narrative in the book; but the same feeling that "mourns o'er the beauty of the Cyclades," often re-emerges in shorter passages.
10100-8{_}1993{_}The fifth and sixth cantos, in spite of the glittering sketch of Gulbeyaz, and tho fawn-like image of Dud, are open to the charge of diffuseness, and the character of Johnson is a failure.
10100-8{_}1994{_}From the seventh to the tenth, the poem decidedly dips, partly because the writer had never been in Russia; then it again rises, and shows no sign of falling off to the end.
10100-8{_}1995{_}No part of the work has more suggestive interest or varied power than some of the later cantos, in which Juan is whirled through the vortex of the fashionable life which Byron knew so well, loved so much, and at last esteemed so little.
10100-8{_}1996{_}There is no richer piece of descriptive writing in his works than that of Newstead (in c.
10100-8{_}1997{_}xiii.
10100-8{_}1998{_}); nor is there any analysis of female character so subtle as that of the Lady Adeline.
10100-8{_}1999{_}Conjectures as to the originals of imaginary portraits, are generally futile; but Miss Millpond--not Donna Inez--is obviously Lady Byron; in Adeline we may suspect that at Genoa he was drawing from the life in the Villa Paradiso; while Aurora Raby seems to be an idealization of La Guiccioli:--    Early in years, and yet more infantine     In figure, she had something of sublime   In eyes, which sadly shone, as seraphs' shine:     All youth--but with an aspect beyond time;   Radiant and grave--us pitying man's decline;     Mournful--but mournful of another's crime,   She look'd as if she sat by Eden's door,   And grieved for those who could return no more.
10100-8{_}2000{_}She was a Catholic, too, sincere, austere,     As far as her own gentle heart allow'd,   And deem'd that fallen worship far more dear,     Perhaps, because 'twas fallen:  her sires were proud   Of deeds and days, when they had fill'd the ear     Of nations, and had never bent or bow'd   To novel power; and, as she was the last,   She held her old faith and old feelings fast.
10100-8{_}2001{_}She gazed upon a world she scarcely knew,     As seeking not to know it; silent, lone,   As grows a flower, thus quietly she grew,     And kept her heart serene within its zone.
10100-8{_}2002{_}Constantly, towards the close of the work, there is an echo of home and country, a half involuntary cry after--    The love of higher things and better days;     Th'unbounded hope, and heavenly ignorance   Of what is call'd the world and the world's ways.
10100-8{_}2003{_}In the concluding stanza of the last completed canto, beginning--    Between two worlds life hovers like a star,     'Twixt night and morn, on the horizon's verge--  we have a condensation of the refrain of the poet's philosophy; but the main drift of the later books is a satire on London society.
10100-8{_}2004{_}There are elements in a great city which may be wrought into something nobler than satire, for all the energies of the age are concentrated where passion is fiercest and thought intensest, amid the myriad sights and sounds of its glare and gloom.
10100-8{_}2005{_}But those scenes, and the actors in them, are apt also to induce the frame of mind in which a prose satirist describes himself as reclining under an arcade of the Pantheon: "Not the Pantheon by the Piazza Navona, where the immortal gods were worshipped--the immortal gods now dead; but the Pantheon in Oxford Street.
10100-8{_}2006{_}Have not Selwyn, and Walpole, and March, and Carlisle figured there?
10100-8{_}2007{_}Has not Prince Florizel flounced through the hall in his rustling domino, and danced there in powdered splendour?
10100-8{_}2008{_}O my companions, I have drunk many a bout with you, and always found 'Vanitas Vanitatum' written on the bottom of the pot."
10100-8{_}2009{_}This is the mind in which _Don Juan_ interprets the universe, and paints the still living court of Florizel and his buffoons.
10100-8{_}2010{_}A "nondescript and ever varying rhyme"--"a versified aurora borealis," half cynical, half Epicurean, it takes a partial though a subtle view of that microcosm on stilts called the great world.
10100-8{_}2011{_}It complains that in the days of old "men made the manners--manners now make men."
10100-8{_}2012{_}It concludes--    Good company's a chess-board, there are kings,   Queens, bishops, knights, rooks, pawns; the world's a game.
10100-8{_}2013{_}It passes from a reflection on "the dreary _fuimus_ of all things here" to the advice--    But "carpe diem," Juan, "carpe, carpe!"
10100-8{_}2014{_}To-morrow sees another race as gay   And transient, and devour'd by the same harpy.
10100-8{_}2015{_}"Life's a poor player,"--then play out the play.
10100-8{_}2016{_}It was the natural conclusion of the foregone stage of Byron's career.
10100-8{_}2017{_}Years had given him power, but they were years in which his energies were largely wasted.
10100-8{_}2018{_}Self-indulgence had not petrified his feeling, but it had thrown wormwood into its springs.
10100-8{_}2019{_}He had learnt to look on existence as a walking shadow, and was strong only with the strength of a sincere despair.
10100-8{_}2020{_}Through life's road, so dim and dirty,   I have dragg'd to three and thirty.
10100-8{_}2021{_}What have those years left to me?
10100-8{_}2022{_}Nothing, except thirty-three.
10100-8{_}2023{_}These lines are the summary of one who had drained the draught of pleasure to the dregs of bitterness.
10100-8{_}2024{_}CHAPTER X.
10100-8{_}2025{_}1821-1824.
10100-8{_}2026{_}POLITICS--THE CARBONARI--EXPEDITION TO GREECE--DEATH.
10100-8{_}2027{_}In leaving Venice for Ravenna, Byron passed from the society of gondoliers and successive sultanas to a comparatively domestic life, with a mistress who at least endeavoured to stimulate some of his higher aspirations, and smiled upon his wearing the sword along with the lyre.
10100-8{_}2028{_}In the last episode of his constantly chequered and too voluptuous career, we have the waking of Sardanapalus realized in the transmutation of the fantastical Harold into a practical strategist, financier, and soldier.
10100-8{_}2029{_}No one ever lived who, in the same space, more thoroughly ran the gauntlet of existence.
10100-8{_}2030{_}Having exhausted all other sources of vitality and intoxication--travel, gallantry, and verse--it remained for the despairing poet to become a hero.
10100-8{_}2031{_}But he was also moved by a public passion, the genuineness of which there is no reasonable ground to doubt.
10100-8{_}2032{_}Like Alfieri and Rousseau, he had taken for his motto, "I am of the opposition;" and, as Dante under a republic called for a monarchy, Byron, under monarchies at home and abroad, called for a commonwealth.
10100-8{_}2033{_}Amid the inconsistencies of his political sentiment, he had been consistent in so much love of liberty as led him to denounce oppression, even when he had no great faith in the oppressed--whether English, or Italians, or Greeks.
10100-8{_}2034{_}Byron regarded the established dynasties of the continent with a sincere hatred.
10100-8{_}2035{_}He talks of the "more than infernal tyranny" of the House of Austria.
10100-8{_}2036{_}To his fancy, as to Shelley's, New England is the star of the future.
10100-8{_}2037{_}Attracted by a strength or rather force of character akin to his own, he worshipped Napoleon, even when driven to confess that "the hero had sunk into a king."
10100-8{_}2038{_}He lamented his overthrow; but, above all, that he was beaten by "three stupid, legitimate old dynasty boobies of regular sovereigns."
10100-8{_}2039{_}"I write in ipecacuanha that the Bourbons are restored."
10100-8{_}2040{_}"What right have we to prescribe laws to France?
10100-8{_}2041{_}Here we are retrograding to the dull, stupid old system, balance of Europe--poising straws on kings' noses, instead of wringing them off."
10100-8{_}2042{_}"The king-times are fast finishing.
10100-8{_}2043{_}There will be blood shed like water, and tears like mist; but the peoples will conquer in the end.
10100-8{_}2044{_}I shall not live to see it, but I foresee it."
10100-8{_}2045{_}"Give me a republic.
10100-8{_}2046{_}Look in the history of the earth--Rome, Greece, Venice, Holland, France, America, our too short Commonwealth--and compare it with what they did under masters."
10100-8{_}2047{_}His serious political verses are all in the strain of the lines on Wellington--    Never had mortal man such opportunity--     Except Napoleon--or abused it more;   You might have freed fallen Europe from the unity     Of tyrants, and been blessed from shore to shore.
10100-8{_}2048{_}An enthusiasm for Italy, which survived many disappointments, dictated some of the most impressive passages of his _Harold_, and inspired the _Lament of Tasso_ and the _Ode on Venice_.
10100-8{_}2049{_}The _Prophecy of Dante_ contains much that has since proved prophetic--    What is there wanting, then, to set thee free,   And show thy beauty in its fullest light?
10100-8{_}2050{_}To make the Alps impassable; and we,   Her sons, may do this with one deed--_Unite_!
10100-8{_}2051{_}His letters reiterate the same idea, in language even more emphatic.
10100-8{_}2052{_}"It is no great matter, supposing that Italy could be liberated, who or what is sacrificed.
10100-8{_}2053{_}It is a grand object--the very poetry of politics; only think--a free Italy!"
10100-8{_}2054{_}Byron acted on his assertion that a man ought to do more for society than write verses.
10100-8{_}2055{_}Mistrusting its leaders, and detesting the wretched lazzaroni, who "would have betrayed themselves and all the world," he yet threw himself heart and soul into the insurrection of 1820, saying, "Whatever I can do by money, means, or person, I will venture freely for their freedom."
10100-8{_}2056{_}He joined the secret society of the Carbonari, wrote an address to the Liberal government set up in Naples, supplied arms and a refuge in his house, which he was prepared to convert into a fortress.
10100-8{_}2057{_}In February, 1821, on the rout of the Neapolitans by the Austrians, the conspiracy was crushed.
10100-8{_}2058{_}Byron, who "had always an idea that it would be bungled," expressed his fear that the country would be thrown back for 500 years into barbarism, and the Countess Guiccioli confessed with tears that the Italians must return to composing and strumming operatic airs.
10100-8{_}2059{_}Carbonarism having collapsed, it of course made way for a reaction; but the encouragement and countenance of the English poet and peer helped to keep alive the smouldering fire that Mazzini fanned into a flame, till Cavour turned it to a practical purpose, and the dreams of the idealists of 1820 were finally realized.
10100-8{_}2060{_}On the failure of the luckless conspiracy, Byron naturally betook himself to history, speculation, satire, and ideas of a journalistic propaganda; but all through, his mind was turning to the renewal of the action which was his destiny.
10100-8{_}2061{_}"If I live ten years longer," he writes in 1822, "you will see that it is not all over with me.
10100-8{_}2062{_}I don't mean in literature, for that is nothing--and I do not think it was my vocation; but I shall do something."
10100-8{_}2063{_}The Greek war of liberation opened a new field for the exercise of his indomitable energy.
10100-8{_}2064{_}This romantic struggle, begun in April, 1821, was carried on for two years with such remarkable success, that at the close of 1822 Greece was beginning to be recognized as an independent state; but in the following months the tide seemed to turn; dissensions broke out among the leaders, the spirit of intrigue seemed to stifle patriotism, and the energies of the insurgents were hampered for want of the sinews of war.
10100-8{_}2065{_}There was a danger of the movement being starved out, and the committee of London sympathizers--of which the poet's intimate friend and frequent correspondent, Mr. Douglas Kinnaird, and Captain Blaquire, were leading promoters--was impressed with the necessity of procuring funds in support of the cause.
10100-8{_}2066{_}With a view to this it seemed of consequence to attach to it some shining name, and men's thoughts almost inevitably turned to Byron.
10100-8{_}2067{_}No other Englishman seemed so fit to be associated with the enterprise as the warlike poet, who had twelve years before linked his fame to that of "grey Marathon" and "Athena's tower," and, more recently immortalized the isles on which he cast so many a longing glance.
10100-8{_}2068{_}Hobhouse broke the subject to him early in the spring of 1823: the committee opened communications in April.
10100-8{_}2069{_}After hesitating through May, in June Byron consented to meet Blaquire at Zante, and, on hearing the results of the captain's expedition to the Morea, to decide on future steps.
10100-8{_}2070{_}His share in this enterprise has been assigned to purely personal and comparatively mean motives.
10100-8{_}2071{_}He was, it is said, disgusted with his periodical, sick of his editor, tired of his mistress, and bent on any change, from China to Peru, that would give him a new theatre for display.
10100-8{_}2072{_}One grows weary of the perpetual half-truths of inveterate detraction.
10100-8{_}2073{_}It is granted that Byron was restless, vain, imperious, never did anything without a desire to shine in the doing of it, and was to a great degree the slave of circumstances.
10100-8{_}2074{_}Had the _Liberal_ proved a lamp to the nations, instead of a mere "red flag flaunted in the face of John Bull," he might have cast anchor at Genoa; but the whole drift of his work and life demonstrates that he was capable on occasion of merging himself in what he conceived to be great causes, especially in their evil days.
10100-8{_}2075{_}Of the Hunts he may have had enough; but the invidious statement about La Guiccioli has no foundation, other than a somewhat random remark of Shelley, and the fact that he left her nothing in his will.
10100-8{_}2076{_}It is distinctly ascertained that she expressly prohibited him from doing so; they continued to correspond to the last, and her affectionate, though unreadable, reminiscences, are sufficient proof that she at no time considered herself to be neglected, injured, or aggrieved.
10100-8{_}2077{_}Byron indeed left Italy in an unsettled state of mind: he spoke of returning in a few months, and as the period for his departure approached, became more and more irresolute.
10100-8{_}2078{_}A presentiment of his death seemed to brood over a mind always superstitious, though never fanatical.
10100-8{_}2079{_}Shortly before his own departure, the Blessingtons were preparing to leave Genoa for England.
10100-8{_}2080{_}On the evening of his farewell call he began to speak of his voyage with despondency, saying, "Here we are all now together; but when and where shall we meet again?
10100-8{_}2081{_}I have a sort of boding that we see each other for the last time, as something tells me I shall never again return from Greece:" after which remark he leant his head on the sofa, and burst into one of his hysterical fits of tears.
10100-8{_}2082{_}The next week was given to preparations for an expedition, which, entered on with mingled motives--sentimental, personal, public--became more real and earnest to Byron at every step he took.
10100-8{_}2083{_}He knew all the vices of the "hereditary bondsmen" among whom he was going, and went among them, with yet unquenched aspirations, but with the bridle of discipline in his hand, resolved to pave the way towards the nation becoming better, by devoting himself to making it free.
10100-8{_}2084{_}On the morning of July 14th (1823) he embarked in the brig "Hercules," with Trelawny, Count Pietro Gamba, who remained with him to the last, Bruno a young Italian doctor, Scott the captain of the vessel, and eight servants, including Fletcher, besides the crew.
10100-8{_}2085{_}They had on board two guns, with other arms and ammunition, five horses, an ample supply of medicines, with 50,000 Spanish dollars in coin and bills.
10100-8{_}2086{_}The start was inauspicious.
10100-8{_}2087{_}A violent squall drove them back to port, and in the course of a last ride with Gamba to Albaro, Byron asked, "Where shall we be in a year?"
10100-8{_}2088{_}On the same day of the same month of 1824 he was carried to the tomb of his ancestors.
10100-8{_}2089{_}They again set sail on the following evening, and in five days reached Leghorn, where the poet received a salutation in verse, addressed to him by Goethe, and replied to it.
10100-8{_}2090{_}Here Mr. Hamilton Brown, a Scotch gentleman with considerable knowledge of Greek affairs, joined the party, and induced them to change their course to Cephalonia, for the purpose of obtaining the advice and assistance of the English resident, Colonel Napier.
10100-8{_}2091{_}The poet occupied himself during the voyage mainly in reading--among other books, Scott's _Life of Swift_, Grimm's _Correspondence_, La Rochefoucauld, and Las Casas--and watching the classic or historic shores which they skirted, especially noting Elba, Soracte, the Straits of Messina, and Etna.
10100-8{_}2092{_}In passing Stromboli he said to Trelawny, "You will see this scene in a fifth canto of _Childe Harold_."
10100-8{_}2093{_}On his companions suggesting that he should write some verses on the spot, he tried to do so, but threw them away, with the remark, "I cannot write poetry at will, as you smoke tobacco."
10100-8{_}2094{_}Trelawny confesses that he was never on shipboard with a better companion, and that a severer test of good fellowship it is impossible to apply.
10100-8{_}2095{_}Together they shot at gulls or empty bottles, and swam every morning in the sea.
10100-8{_}2096{_}Early in August they reached their destination.
10100-8{_}2097{_}Coming in sight of the Morea, the poet said to Trelawny, "I feel as if the eleven long years of bitterness I have passed through, since I was here, were taken from my shoulders, and I was scudding through the Greek Archipelago with old Bathurst in his frigate."
10100-8{_}2098{_}Byron remained at or about Cephalonia till the close of the year.
10100-8{_}2099{_}Not long after his arrival he made an excursion to Ithaca, and, visiting the monastery at Vathi, was received by the abbot with great ceremony, which, in a fit of irritation, brought on by a tiresome ride on a mule, he returned with unusual discourtesy; but next morning, on his giving a donation to their alms-box, he was dismissed with the blessing of the monks.
10100-8{_}2100{_}"If this isle were mine," he declared on his way back, "I would break my staff and bury my book."
10100-8{_}2101{_}A little later, Brown and Trelawny being sent off with letters to the provisional government, the former returned with some Greek emissaries to London, to negotiate a loan; the latter attached himself to Odysseus, the chief of the republican party at Athens, and never again saw Byron alive.
10100-8{_}2102{_}The poet, after spending a month on board the "Hercules," dismissed the vessel, and hired a house for Gamba and himself at Metaxata, a healthy village about four miles from the capital of the island.
10100-8{_}2103{_}Meanwhile, Blaquire, neglecting his appointment at Zante, had gone to Corfu, and thence to England.
10100-8{_}2104{_}Colonel Napier being absent from Cephalonia, Byron had some pleasant social intercourse with his deputy, but, unable to get from him any authoritative information, was left without advice, to be besieged by letters and messages from the factions.
10100-8{_}2105{_}Among these there were brought to him hints that the Greeks wanted a king, and he is reported to have said, "If they make me the offer, I will perhaps not reject it."
10100-8{_}2106{_}The position would doubtless have been acceptable to a man who never--amid his many self-deceptions--affected to deny that he was ambitious: and who can say what might not have resulted for Greece, had the poet lived to add lustre to her crown?
10100-8{_}2107{_}In the meantime, while faring more frugally than a day-labourer, he yet surrounded himself with a show of royal state, had his servants armed with gilt helmets, and gathered around him a body-guard of Suliotes.
10100-8{_}2108{_}These wild mercenaries becoming turbulent, he was obliged to despatch them to Mesolonghi, then threatened with siege by the Turks and anxiously waiting relief.
10100-8{_}2109{_}During his residence at Cephalonia, Byron was gratified by the interest evinced in him by the English residents.
10100-8{_}2110{_}Among these the physician, Dr. Kennedy, a worthy Scotchman, who imagined himself to be a theologian with a genius for conversion, was conducting a series of religious meetings at Argostoli, when the poet expressed a wish to be present at one of them.
10100-8{_}2111{_}After listening, it is said, to a set of discourses that occupied the greater part of twelve hours, he seems, for one reason or another, to have felt called on to enter the lists, and found himself involved in the series of controversial dialogues afterwards published in a substantial book.
10100-8{_}2112{_}This volume, interesting in several respects, is one of the most charming examples of unconscious irony in the language, and it is matter of regret that our space does not admit of the abridgment of several of its pages.
10100-8{_}2113{_}They bear testimony, on the one hand, to Byron's capability of patience, and frequent sweetness of temper under trial; on the other, to Kennedy's utter want of humour, and to his courageous honesty.
10100-8{_}2114{_}The curiously confronted interlocutors, in the course of the missionary and subsequent private meetings, ran over most of the ground debated between opponents and apologists of the Calvinistic faith, which Kennedy upheld without stint.
10100-8{_}2115{_}The _Conversations_ add little to what we already know of Byron's religious opinions; nor is it easy to say where he ceases to be serious and begins to banter, or vice versa.
10100-8{_}2116{_}He evidently wished to show that in argument he was good at fence, and could handle a theologian as skilfully as a foil.
10100-8{_}2117{_}At the same time he wished if possible, though, as appears, in vain, to get some light on a subject with regard to which in his graver moods he was often exercised.
10100-8{_}2118{_}On some points he is explicit.
10100-8{_}2119{_}He makes an unequivocal protest against the doctrines of eternal punishment and infant damnation, saying that if the rest of mankind were to be damned, he "would rather keep them company than creep into heaven alone."
10100-8{_}2120{_}On questions of inspiration, and the deeper problems of human life, he is less distinct, being naturally inclined to a speculative necessitarianism, and disposed to admit original depravity; but he did not see his way out of the maze through the Atonement, and held that prayer had only significance as a devotional affection of the heart.
10100-8{_}2121{_}Byron showed a remarkable familiarity with the Scriptures, and with parts of Barrow, Chillingworth, and Stillingfleet; but on Kennedy's lending for his edification Boston's _Fourfold State_, he returned it with the remark that it was too deep for him.
10100-8{_}2122{_}On another occasion he said, "Do you know I am nearly reconciled to St. Paul, for he says there is no difference between the Jews and the Greeks?
10100-8{_}2123{_}and I am exactly of the same opinion, for the character of both is equally vile."
10100-8{_}2124{_}The good Scotchman's religious self-confidence is throughout free from intellectual pride; and his own confession, "This time I suspect his lordship had the best of it," might perhaps be applied to the whole discussion.
10100-8{_}2125{_}Critics who have little history and less war have been accustomed to attribute Byron's lingering at Cephalonia to indolence and indecision; they write as if he ought on landing on Greek soil to have put himself at the head of an army and stormed Constantinople.
10100-8{_}2126{_}Those who know more, confess that the delay was deliberate, and that it was judicious.
10100-8{_}2127{_}The Hellenic uprising was animated by the spirit of a "lion after slumber," but it had the heads of a Hydra hissing and tearing at one another.
10100-8{_}2128{_}The chiefs who defended the country by their arms, compromised her by their arguments, and some of her best fighters were little better than pirates and bandits.
10100-8{_}2129{_}Greece was a prey to factions--republican, monarchic, aristocratic--representing naval, military, and territorial interests, and each beset by the adventurers who flock round every movement, only representing their own.
10100-8{_}2130{_}During the first two years of success they were held in embryo; during the later years of disaster, terminated by the allies at Navarino, they were buried; during the interlude of Byron's residence, when the foes were like hounds in the leash, waiting for a renewal of the struggle, they were rampant.
10100-8{_}2131{_}Had he joined any one of them he would have degraded himself to the level of a mere condottiere, and helped to betray the common cause.
10100-8{_}2132{_}Beset by solicitations to go to Athens, to the Morea, to Acarnania, he resolutely held apart, biding his time, collecting information, making himself known as a man of affairs, endeavouring to conciliate rival clamants for pension or place, and carefully watching the tide of war.
10100-8{_}2133{_}Numerous anecdotes of the period relate to acts of public or private benevolence, which endeared him to the population of the island; but he was on the alert against being fleeced or robbed.
10100-8{_}2134{_}"The bulk of the English," writes Colonel Napier, "came expecting to find the Peloponnesus filled with Plutarch's men, and returned thinking the inhabitants of Newgate more moral.
10100-8{_}2135{_}Lord Byron judged the Greeks fairly, and knew that allowance must be made for emancipated slaves."
10100-8{_}2136{_}Among other incidents we hear of his passing a group, who were "shrieking and howling as in Ireland" over some men buried in the fall of a bank; he snatched a spade, began to dig, and threatened to horsewhip the peasants unless they followed his example.
10100-8{_}2137{_}On November 30th he despatched to the central government a remarkable state paper, in which he dwells on the fatal calamity of a civil war, and says that unless union and order are established all hopes of a loan--which being every day more urgent, he was in letters to England constantly pressing--are at an end.
10100-8{_}2138{_}"I desire," he concluded, "the well being of Greece, and nothing else.
10100-8{_}2139{_}I will do all I can to secure it; but I will never consent that the English public be deceived as to the real state of affairs.
10100-8{_}2140{_}You have fought gloriously; act honourably towards your fellow-citizens and the world, and it will then no more be said, as has been repeated for two thousand years, with the Roman historians, that Philopoemen was the last of the Grecians."
10100-8{_}2141{_}Prince Alexander Mavrocordatos--the most prominent of the practical patriotic leaders--having been deposed from the presidency, was sent to regulate the affairs of Western Greece, and was now on his way with a fleet to relieve Mesolonghi, in attempting which the brave Marco Bozzaris had previously fallen.
10100-8{_}2142{_}In a letter, opening communication with a man for whom he always entertained a high esteem, Byron writes, "Colonel Stanhope has arrived from London, charged by our committee to act in concert with me.... Greece is at present placed between three measures--either to reconquer her liberty, to become a dependence of the sovereigns of Europe, or to return to a Turkish province.
10100-8{_}2143{_}She has the choice only of these three alternatives.
10100-8{_}2144{_}Civil war is but a road that leads to the two latter."
10100-8{_}2145{_}At length the long looked-for fleet arrived, and the Turkish squadron, with the loss of a treasure-ship, retired up the Gulf of Lepanto.
10100-8{_}2146{_}Mavrocordatos on entering Mesolonghi lost no time in inviting the poet to join him, and placed a brig at his disposal, adding, "I need not tell you to what a pitch your presence is desired by everybody, or what a prosperous direction it will give to all our affairs.
10100-8{_}2147{_}Your counsels will be listened to like oracles."
10100-8{_}2148{_}At the same date Stanhope writes, "The people in the streets are looking forward to his lordship's arrival as they would to the coming of the Messiah."
10100-8{_}2149{_}Byron was unable to start in the ship sent for him; but in spite of medical warnings, a few days later, i.e.
10100-8{_}2150{_}December 28th, he embarked in a small fast-sailing sloop called a mistico, while the servants and baggage were stowed in another and larger vessel under the charge of Count Gamba.
10100-8{_}2151{_}From Gamba's graphic account of the voyage we may take the following:--"We sailed together till after ten at night; the wind favourable, a clear sky, the air fresh, but not sharp.
10100-8{_}2152{_}Our sailors sang alternately patriotic songs, monotonous indeed, but to persons in our situation extremely touching, and we took part in them.
10100-8{_}2153{_}We were all, but Lord Byron particularly, in excellent spirits.
10100-8{_}2154{_}The mistico sailed the fastest.
10100-8{_}2155{_}When the waves divided us, and our voices could no longer reach each other, we made signals by firing pistols and carbines.
10100-8{_}2156{_}To-morrow we meet at Mesolonghi--to morrow.
10100-8{_}2157{_}Thus, full of confidence and spirits, we sailed along.
10100-8{_}2158{_}At twelve we were out of sight of each other."
10100-8{_}2159{_}Byron's vessel, separated from her consort, came into the close proximity of a Turkish frigate, and had to take refuge among the Scrofes' rocks.
10100-8{_}2160{_}Emerging thence, he attained a small seaport of Acarnania, called Dragomestri, whence sallying forth on the 2nd of January under the convoy of some Greek gunboats, he was nearly wrecked.
10100-8{_}2161{_}On the 4th Byron made, when violently heated, an imprudent plunge in the sea, and was never afterwards free from a pain in his bones.
10100-8{_}2162{_}On the 5th he arrived at Mesolonghi, and was received with salvoes of musketry and music.
10100-8{_}2163{_}Gamba was waiting him.
10100-8{_}2164{_}His vessel, the "Bombarda," had been taken by the Ottoman frigate, but the captain of the latter, recognizing the Count as having formerly saved his life in the Black Sea, made interest in his behalf with Yussuf Pasha at Patras, and obtained his discharge.
10100-8{_}2165{_}In recompense, the poet subsequently sent to the Pasha some Turkish prisoners, with a letter requesting him to endeavour to mitigate the inhumanities of the war.
10100-8{_}2166{_}Byron brought to the Greeks at Mesolonghi the 4000_l_.
10100-8{_}2167{_}of his personal loan (applied, in the first place, to defraying the expenses of the fleet), with the spell of his name and presence.
10100-8{_}2168{_}He was shortly afterwards appointed to the command of the intended expedition against Lepanto, and, with this view, again took into his pay five hundred Suliotes.
10100-8{_}2169{_}An approaching general assembly to organize the forces of the west, had brought together a motley crew, destitute, discontented, and more likely to wage war upon each other than on their enemies.
10100-8{_}2170{_}Byron's closest associates during the ensuing months, were the engineer Parry, an energetic artilleryman, "extremely active, and of strong practical talents," who had travelled in America, and Colonel Stanhope (afterwards Lord Harrington) equally with himself devoted to the emancipation of Greece, but at variance about the means of achieving it.
10100-8{_}2171{_}Stanhope, a moral enthusiast of the stamp of Kennedy, beset by the fallacy of religious missions, wished to cover the Morea with Wesleyan tracts, and liberate the country by the agency of the Press.
10100-8{_}2172{_}He had imported a converted blacksmith, with a cargo of Bibles, types, and paper, who on 20_l_.
10100-8{_}2173{_}a year, undertook to accomplish the reform.
10100-8{_}2174{_}Byron, backed by the good sense of Mavrocordatos, proposed to make cartridges of the tracts, and small shot of the type; he did not think that the turbulent tribes were ripe for freedom of the press, and had begun to regard Republicanism itself as a matter of secondary moment.
10100-8{_}2175{_}The disputant allies in the common cause occupied each a flat of the same small house, the soldier by profession was bent on writing the Turks down, the poet on fighting them down, holding that "the work of the sword must precede that of the pen, and that camps must be the training schools of freedom."
10100-8{_}2176{_}Their altercations were sometimes fierce--"Despot!"
10100-8{_}2177{_}cried Stanhope, "after professing liberal principles from boyhood, you when called to act prove yourself a Turk."
10100-8{_}2178{_}"Radical!"
10100-8{_}2179{_}retorted Byron, "if I had held up my finger I could have crushed your press,"--but this did not prevent the recognition by each of them of the excellent qualities of the other.
10100-8{_}2180{_}Ultimately Stanhope went to Athens, and allied himself with Trelawny and Odysseus and the party of the Left.
10100-8{_}2181{_}Nothing can be more statesmanlike than some of Byron's papers of this and the immediately preceding period; nothing more admirable than the spirit which inspires them.
10100-8{_}2182{_}He had come into the heart of a revolution, exposed to the same perils as those which had wrecked the similar movement in Italy.
10100-8{_}2183{_}Neither trusting too much nor distrusting too much, with a clear head and a good will he set about enforcing a series of excellent measures.
10100-8{_}2184{_}From first to last he was engaged in denouncing dissension, in advocating unity, in doing everything that man could do to concentrate and utilize the disorderly elements with which he had to work.
10100-8{_}2185{_}He occupied himself in repairing fortifications, managing ships, restraining licence, promoting courtesy between the foes, and regulating the disposal of the sinews of war.
10100-8{_}2186{_}On the morning of the 22nd of January, his last birthday, he came from his room to Stanhope's, and said, smiling, "You were complaining that I never write any poetry now," and read the familiar stanzas beginning--    'Tis time this heart should be unmoved,  and ending--    Seek out--less often sought than found--     A soldier's grave, for thee the best;   Then look around, and choose thy ground,         And take thy rest.
10100-8{_}2187{_}High thoughts, high resolves; but the brain that was over-tasked, and the frame that was outworn, would be tasked and worn little longer.
10100-8{_}2188{_}The lamp of a life that had burnt too fiercely was flickering to its close.
10100-8{_}2189{_}"If we are not taken off with the sword," he writes on February 5th, "we are like to march off with an ague in this mud basket; and, to conclude with a very bad pun, better _martially_ than _marsh-ally_.
10100-8{_}2190{_}The dykes of Holland when broken down are the deserts of Arabia, in comparison with Mesolonghi."
10100-8{_}2191{_}In April, when it was too late, Stanhope wrote from Salona, in Phocis, imploring him not to sacrifice health, and perhaps life, "in that bog."
10100-8{_}2192{_}Byron's house stood in the midst of the exhalations of a muddy creek, and his natural irritability was increased by a more than usually long ascetic regimen.
10100-8{_}2193{_}From the day of his arrival in Greece he discarded animal food and lived mainly on toast, vegetables, and cheese, olives and light wine, at the rate of forty paras a day.
10100-8{_}2194{_}In spite of his strength of purpose, his temper was not always proof against the rapacity and turbulence by which he was surrounded.
10100-8{_}2195{_}About the middle of February, when the artillery had been got into readiness for the attack on Lepanto--the northern, as Patras was the southern, gate of the gulf, still in the hands of the Turks--the expedition was thrown back by the unexpected rising of the Suliotes.
10100-8{_}2196{_}These peculiarly Irish Greeks, chronically seditious by nature, were on this occasion, as afterwards appeared, stirred up by emissaries of Colocatroni, who, though assuming the position of the rival of Mavrocordatos, was simply a brigand on a large scale in the Morca.
10100-8{_}2197{_}Exasperation at this mutiny, and the vexation of having to abandon a cherished scheme, seem to have been the immediately provoking causes of a violent convulsive fit which, on the evening of the 15th, attacked the poet, and endangered his life.
10100-8{_}2198{_}Next day he was better, but complained of weight in the head; and the doctors applying leeches too close to the temporal artery, he was bled till he fainted.
10100-8{_}2199{_}And now occurred the last of those striking incidents so frequent in his life, in reference to which we may quote the joint testimony of two witnesses.
10100-8{_}2200{_}Colonel Stanhope writes, "Soon after his dreadful paroxysm, when he was lying on his sick-bed, with his whole nervous system completely shaken, the mutinous Suliotes, covered with dirt and splendid attires, broke into his apartment, brandishing their costly arms and loudly demanding their rights.
10100-8{_}2201{_}Lord Byron, electrified by this unexpected act, seemed to recover from his sickness; and the more the Suliotes raged, the more his calm courage triumphed.
10100-8{_}2202{_}The scene was truly sublime."
10100-8{_}2203{_}"It is impossible," says Count Gamba, "to do justice to the coolness and magnanimity which he displayed upon every trying occasion.
10100-8{_}2204{_}Upon trifling occasions he was certainly irritable; but the aspect of danger calmed him in an instant, and restored him the free exercise of all the powers of his noble nature.
10100-8{_}2205{_}A more undaunted man in the hour of peril never breathed."
10100-8{_}2206{_}A few days later, the riot being renewed, the disorderly crew were, on payment of their arrears, finally dismissed; but several of the English artificers under Parry left about the same time, in fear of their lives.
10100-8{_}2207{_}On the 4th, the last of the long list of Byron's letters to Moore resents, with some bitterness, the hasty acceptance of a rumour that he had been quietly writing _Don Juan_ in some Ionian island.
10100-8{_}2208{_}At the same date he writes to Kennedy, "I am not unaware of the precarious state of my health.
10100-8{_}2209{_}But it is proper I should remain in Greece, and it were better to die doing something than nothing."
10100-8{_}2210{_}Visions of enlisting Europe and America on behalf of the establishment of a new state, that might in course of time develope itself over the realm of Alexander, floated and gleamed in his fancy; but in his practical daily procedure the poet took as his text the motto "festina lente," insisted on solid ground under his feet, and had no notion of sailing balloons over the sea.
10100-8{_}2211{_}With this view he discouraged Stanhope's philanthropic and propagandist paper, the _Telegrapho_, and disparaged Dr. Mayor, its Swiss editor, saying, "Of all petty tyrants he is one of the pettiest, as are most demagogues."
10100-8{_}2212{_}Byron had none of the Sclavonic leanings, and almost personal hatred of Ottoman rule, of some of our statesmen; but he saw on what side lay the forces and the hopes of the future.
10100-8{_}2213{_}"I cannot calculate," he said to Gamba, during one of their latest rides together, "to what a height Greece may rise.
10100-8{_}2214{_}Hitherto it has been a subject for the hymns and elegies of fanatics and enthusiasts; but now it will draw the attention of the politician.... At present there is little difference, in many respects, between Greeks and Turks, nor could there be; but the latter must, in the common course of events, decline in power; and the former must as inevitably become better....
10100-8{_}2215{_}The English Government deceived itself at first in thinking it possible to maintain the Turkish Empire in its integrity; but it cannot be done, that unwieldy mass is already putrified, and must dissolve.
10100-8{_}2216{_}If anything like an equilibrium is to be upheld, Greece must be supported."
10100-8{_}2217{_}These words have been well characterized as prophetic.
10100-8{_}2218{_}During this time Byron rallied in health, and displayed much of his old spirit, vivacity, and humour, took part in such of his favourite amusements as circumstances admitted, fencing, shooting, riding, and playing with his pet dog Lion.
10100-8{_}2219{_}The last of his recorded practical jokes is his rolling about cannon balls, and shaking the rafters, to frighten Parry in the room below with the dread of an earthquake.
10100-8{_}2220{_}Towards the close of the month, after being solicited to accompany Mavrocordatos, to share the governorship of the Morea, he made an appointment to meet Colonel Stanhope and Odysseus at Salona, but was prevented from keeping it by violent floods which blocked up the communication.
10100-8{_}2221{_}On the 30th he was presented with the freedom of the city of Mesolonghi.
10100-8{_}2222{_}On the 3rd of April he intervened to prevent an Italian private, guilty of theft, from being flogged by order of some German officers.
10100-8{_}2223{_}On the 9th, exhilarated by a letter from Mrs. Leigh with good accounts of her own and Ada's health, he took a long ride with Gamba and a few of the remaining Suliotes, and after being violently heated, and then drenched in a heavy shower, persisted in returning home in a boat, remarking with a laugh, in answer to a remonstrance, "I should make a pretty soldier if I were to care for such a trifle."
10100-8{_}2224{_}It soon became apparent that he had caught his death.
10100-8{_}2225{_}Almost immediately on his return, he was seized with shiverings and violent pain.
10100-8{_}2226{_}The next day he rose as usual, and had his last ride in the olive woods.
10100-8{_}2227{_}On the 11th a rheumatic fever set in.
10100-8{_}2228{_}On the 14th, Bruno's skill being exhausted, it was proposed to call Dr. Thomas from Zante, but a hurricane prevented any ship being sent.
10100-8{_}2229{_}On the 15th, another physician, Mr. Milligen, suggested bleeding to allay the fever, but Byron held out against it, quoting Dr. Reid to the effect that "less slaughter is effected by the lance than the lancet--that minute instrument of mighty mischief;" and saying to Bruno, "If my hour is come I shall die, whether I lose my blood or keep it."
10100-8{_}2230{_}Next morning Milligen induced him to yield, by a suggestion of the possible loss of his reason.
10100-8{_}2231{_}Throwing out his arm, he cried, "There!
10100-8{_}2232{_}you are, I see, a d----d set of butchers.
10100-8{_}2233{_}Take away as much blood as you like, and have done with it."
10100-8{_}2234{_}The remedy, repeated on the following day with blistering, was either too late or ill-advised.
10100-8{_}2235{_}On the 18th he saw more doctors, but was manifestly sinking, amid the tears and lamentations of attendants who could not understand each other's language.
10100-8{_}2236{_}In his last hours his delirium bore him to the field of arms.
10100-8{_}2237{_}He fancied he was leading the attack on Lepanto, and was heard exclaiming, "Forwards!
10100-8{_}2238{_}forwards!
10100-8{_}2239{_}follow me!"
10100-8{_}2240{_}Who is not reminded of another death-bed, not remote in time from his, and the _Tte d'arme_ of the great Emperor who with the great Poet divided the wonder of Europe?
10100-8{_}2241{_}The stormy vision passed, and his thoughts reverted home.
10100-8{_}2242{_}"Go to my sister," he faltered out to Fletcher; "tell her--go to Lady Byron--you will see her, and say"--nothing more could be heard but broken ejaculations: "Augusta--Ada--my sister, my child.
10100-8{_}2243{_}Io lascio qualche cosa di caro nel mondo.
10100-8{_}2244{_}For the rest, I am content to die."
10100-8{_}2245{_}At six on the evening of the 18th he uttered his last words, "[Greek: _Dei me nun katheudein_];" and on the 19th he passed away.
10100-8{_}2246{_}Never perhaps was there such a national lamentation.
10100-8{_}2247{_}By order of Mavrocordatos, thirty-seven guns--one for each year of the poet's life-- were fired from the battery, and answered by the Turks from Patras with an exultant volley.
10100-8{_}2248{_}All offices, tribunals, and shops were shut, and a general mourning for twenty-one days proclaimed.
10100-8{_}2249{_}Stanhope wrote, on hearing the news, "England has lost her brightest genius--Greece her noblest friend;" and Trelawny, on coming to Mesolonghi, heard nothing in the streets but "Byron is dead!"
10100-8{_}2250{_}like a bell tolling through the silence and the gloom.
10100-8{_}2251{_}Intending contributors to the cause of Greece turned back when they heard the tidings, that seemed to them to mean she was headless.
10100-8{_}2252{_}Her cities contended for the body, as of old for the birth of a poet.
10100-8{_}2253{_}Athens wished him to rest in the Temple of Theseus.
10100-8{_}2254{_}The funeral service was performed at Mesolonghi.
10100-8{_}2255{_}But on the 2nd of May the embalmed remains left Zante, and on the 29th arrived in the Downs.
10100-8{_}2256{_}His relatives applied for permission to have them interred in Westminster Abbey, but it was refused; and on the 16th July they were conveyed to the village church of Hucknall.
10100-8{_}2257{_}CHAPTER XI.
10100-8{_}2258{_}CHARACTERISTICS, AND PLACE IN LITERATURE.
10100-8{_}2259{_}Lord Jeffrey at the close of a once-famous review quaintly laments: "The tuneful quartos of Southey are already little better than lumber, and the rich melodies of Keats and Shelley, and the fantastical emphasis of Wordsworth, and the plebeian pathos of Crabbe, are melting fast from the field of our vision.
10100-8{_}2260{_}The novels of Scott have put out his poetry, and the blazing star of Byron himself is receding from its place of pride."
10100-8{_}2261{_}Of the poets of the early part of this century, Lord John Russell thought Byron the greatest, then Scott, then Moore.
10100-8{_}2262{_}"Such an opinion," wrote a _National_ reviewer, in 1860, "is not worth a refutation; we only smile at it."
10100-8{_}2263{_}Nothing in the history of literature is more curious than the shifting of the standard of excellence, which so perplexes criticism.
10100-8{_}2264{_}But the most remarkable feature of the matter is the frequent return to power of the once discarded potentates.
10100-8{_}2265{_}Byron is resuming his place: his spirit has come again to our atmosphere; and every budding critic, as in 1820, is impelled to pronounce a verdict on his genius and character.
10100-8{_}2266{_}The present times are, in many respects, an aftermath of the first quarter of the century, which was an era of revolt, of doubt, of storm.
10100-8{_}2267{_}There succeeded an era of exhaustion, of quiescence, of reflection.
10100-8{_}2268{_}The first years of the third quarter saw a revival of turbulence and agitation; and, more than our fathers, we are inclined to sympathize with our grandfathers.
10100-8{_}2269{_}Macaulay has popularized the story of the change of literary dynasty which in our island marked the close of the last, and the first two decades of the present, hundred years.
10100-8{_}2270{_}The corresponding artistic revolt on the continent was closely connected with changes in the political world.
10100-8{_}2271{_}The originators of the romantic literature in Italy, for the most part, died in Spielberg or in exile.
10100-8{_}2272{_}The same revolution which levelled the Bastille, and converted Versailles and the Trianon--the classic school in stone and terrace--into a moral Herculaneum and Pompeii, drove the models of the so-called Augustan ages into a museum of antiquarians.
10100-8{_}2273{_}In our own country, the movement initiated by Chatterton, Cowper, and Burns, was carried out by two classes of great writers.
10100-8{_}2274{_}They agreed in opposing freedom to formality; in substituting for the old, new aims and methods; in preferring a grain of mother wit to a peck of clerisy.
10100-8{_}2275{_}They broke with the old school, as Protestantism broke with the old Church; but, like the sects, they separated again.
10100-8{_}2276{_}Wordsworth, Southey, and Coleridge, while refusing to acknowledge the literary precedents of the past, submitted themselves to a self-imposed law.
10100-8{_}2277{_}The partialities of their maturity were towards things settled and regulated; their favourite virtues, endurance and humility; their conformity to established institutions was the basis of a new Conservatism.
10100-8{_}2278{_}The others were the Radicals of the movement: they practically acknowledged no law but their own inspiration.
10100-8{_}2279{_}Dissatisfied with the existing order, their sympathies were with strong will and passion and defiant independence.
10100-8{_}2280{_}These found their master-types in Shelley and in Byron.
10100-8{_}2281{_}A reaction is always an extreme.
10100-8{_}2282{_}Lollards, Puritans, Covenanters, were in some respects nauseous antidotes to ecclesiastical corruption.
10100-8{_}2283{_}The ruins of the Scotch cathedrals and of the French nobility are warnings at once against the excess that provokes and the excess that avenges.
10100-8{_}2284{_}The revolt against the _ancien rgime_ in letters made possible the Ode that is the high-tide mark of modern English inspiration, but it was parodied in page on page of maundering rusticity.
10100-8{_}2285{_}Byron saw the danger, but was borne headlong by the rapids.
10100-8{_}2286{_}Hence the anomalous contrast between his theories and his performance.
10100-8{_}2287{_}Both Wordsworth and Byron were bitten by Rousseau; but the former is, at furthest, a Girondin.
10100-8{_}2288{_}The latter, acting like Danton on the motto "L'audace, l'audace, toujours l'audace," sighs after _Henri Quatre et Gabrille_.
10100-8{_}2289{_}There is more of the spirit of the French Revolution in _Don Juan_ than in all the works of the author's contemporaries; but his criticism is that of Boileau, and when deliberate is generally absurd.
10100-8{_}2290{_}He never recognized the meaning of the artistic movement of his age, and overvalued those of his works which the Unities helped to destroy.
10100-8{_}2291{_}He hailed Gifford as his Magnus Apollo, and put Rogers next to Scott in his comical pyramid.
10100-8{_}2292{_}"Chaucer," he writes, "I think obscene and contemptible."
10100-8{_}2293{_}He could see no merit in Spenser, preferred Tasso to Milton, and called the old English dramatists "mad and turbid mountebanks."
10100-8{_}2294{_}In the same spirit he writes: "In the time of Pope it was all Horace, now it is all Claudian."
10100-8{_}2295{_}He saw--what fanatics had begun to deny--that Pope was a great writer, and the "angel of reasonableness," the strong common sense of both was a link between them; but the expressions he uses during his controversy with Bowles look like jests, till we are convinced of his earnestness by his anger.
10100-8{_}2296{_}"Neither time, nor distance, nor grief, nor age can ever diminish my veneration for him who is the great moral poet of all times, of all climes, of all feelings, and of all stages of existence....
10100-8{_}2297{_}Your whole generation are not worth a canto of the _Dunciad_, or anything that is his."
10100-8{_}2298{_}All the while he was himself writing prose and verse, in grasp if not in vigour as far beyond the stretch of Pope, as Pope is in "worth and wit and sense" removed above his mimics.
10100-8{_}2299{_}The point of the paradox is not merely that he deserted, but that he sometimes imitated his model, and when he did so, failed.
10100-8{_}2300{_}Macaulay's judgment, that "personal taste led him to the eighteenth century, thirst for praise to the nineteenth," is quite at fault.
10100-8{_}2301{_}There can be no doubt that Byron loved praise as much as he affected to despise it.
10100-8{_}2302{_}His note, on reading the _Quarterly_ on his dramas, "I am the most unpopular man in England," is like the cry of a child under chastisement; but he had little affinity, moral or artistic, with the spirit of our so-called Augustans, and his determination to admire them was itself rebellious.
10100-8{_}2303{_}Again we are reminded of his phrase, "I am of the opposition."
10100-8{_}2304{_}His vanity and pride were perpetually struggling for the mastery, and though he thirsted for popularity he was bent on compelling it; so he warred with the literary impulse of which he was the child.
10100-8{_}2305{_}Byron has no relation to the master-minds whose works reflect a nation or an era, and who keep their own secrets.
10100-8{_}2306{_}His verse and prose is alike biographical, and the inequalities of his style are those of his career.
10100-8{_}2307{_}He lived in a glass case, and could not hide himself by his habit of burning blue lights.
10100-8{_}2308{_}He was too great to do violence to his nature, which was not great enough to be really consistent.
10100-8{_}2309{_}It was thus natural for him to pose as the spokesman of two ages--as a critic and as an author; and of two orders of society--as a peer, and as a poet of revolt.
10100-8{_}2310{_}Sincere in both, he could never forget the one character in the other.
10100-8{_}2311{_}To the last, he was an aristocrat in sentiment, a democrat in opinion.
10100-8{_}2312{_}"Vulgarity," he writes with a pithy half-truth, "is far worse than downright black guardism; for the latter comprehends wit, humour, and strong sense at times, while the former is a sad abortive attempt at all things, signifying nothing."
10100-8{_}2313{_}He could never reconcile himself to the English radicals; and it has been acutely remarked, that part of his final interest in Greece lay in the fact that he found it a country of classic memories, "where a man might be the champion of liberty without soiling himself in the arena."
10100-8{_}2314{_}He owed much of his early influence to the fact of his moving in the circles of rank and fashion; but though himself steeped in the prejudices of caste, he struck at them at times with fatal force.
10100-8{_}2315{_}Aristocracy is the individual asserting a vital distinction between itself and "the muck o' the world."
10100-8{_}2316{_}Byron's heroes all rebel against the associative tendency of the nineteenth century; they are self-worshippers at war with society; but most of them come to bad ends.
10100-8{_}2317{_}He maligned himself in those caricatures, and has given more of himself in describing one whom with special significance we call a brother poet.
10100-8{_}2318{_}"Allen," he writes in 1813, "has lent me a quantity of Burns's unpublished letters.... What an antithetical mind!--tenderness, roughness--delicacy, coarseness-- sentiment, sensuality--soaring and grovelling--dirt and deity--all mixed up in that one compound of inspired clay!"
10100-8{_}2319{_}We have only to add to these antitheses, in applying them with slight modification to the writer.
10100-8{_}2320{_}Byron had, on occasion, more self-control than Burns, who yielded to every thirst or gust, and could never have lived the life of the soldier at Mesolonghi; but partly owing to meanness, partly to a sound instinct, his memory has been more severely dealt with.
10100-8{_}2321{_}The fact of his being a nobleman helped to make him famous, but it also helped to make him hated.
10100-8{_}2322{_}No doubt it half spoiled him in making him a show; and the circumstance has suggested the remark of a humourist, that it is as hard for a lord to be a perfect gentleman as for a camel to pass through the needle's eye.
10100-8{_}2323{_}But it also exposed to the rancours of jealousy a man who had nearly everything but domestic happiness to excite that most corroding of literary passions; and when he got out of gear he became the quarry of Spenser's "blatant beast."
10100-8{_}2324{_}On the other hand, Burns was, beneath his disgust at Holy Fairs and Willies, sincerely reverential; much of _Don Juan_ would have seemed to him "an atheist's laugh," and--a more certain superiority--he was absolutely frank.
10100-8{_}2325{_}Byron, like Pope, was given to playing monkey-like tricks, mostly harmless, but offensive to their victims.
10100-8{_}2326{_}His peace of mind was dependent on what people would say of him, to a degree unusual even in the irritable race; and when they spoke ill he was, again like Pope, essentially vindictive.
10100-8{_}2327{_}The _Bards and Reviewers_ beats about, where the lines to Atticus transfix with Philoctetes' arrows; but they are due to a like impulse.
10100-8{_}2328{_}Byron affected to contemn the world; but, say what he would, he cared too much for it.
10100-8{_}2329{_}He had a genuine love of solitude as an alterative; but he could not subsist without society, and, Shelley tells us, wherever he went, became the nucleus of it.
10100-8{_}2330{_}He sprang up again when flung to the earth, but he never attained to the disdain he desired.
10100-8{_}2331{_}We find him at once munificent and careful about money; calmly asleep amid a crowd of trembling sailors, yet never going to ride without a nervous caution; defying augury, yet seriously disturbed by a gipsy's prattle.
10100-8{_}2332{_}He could be the most genial of comrades, the most considerate of masters, and he secured the devotion of his servants, as of his friends; but he was too overbearing to form many equal friendships, and apt to be ungenerous to his real rivals.
10100-8{_}2333{_}His shifting attitude towards Lady Byron, his wavering purposes, his impulsive acts, are a part of the character we trace through all his life and work,--a strange mixture of magnanimity and brutality, of laughter and tears, consistent in nothing but his passion and his pride, yet redeeming all his defects by his graces, and wearing a greatness that his errors can only half obscure.
10100-8{_}2334{_}Alternately the idol and the horror of his contemporaries, Byron was, during his life, feared and respected as "the grand Napoleon of the realms of rhyme."
10100-8{_}2335{_}His works were the events of the literary world.
10100-8{_}2336{_}The chief among them were translated into French, German, Italian, Danish, Polish, Russian, Spanish.
10100-8{_}2337{_}On the publication of Moore's _Life_, Lord Macaulay had no hesitation in referring to Byron as "the most celebrated Englishman of the nineteenth century."
10100-8{_}2338{_}Nor have we now; but in the interval between 1840-1870, it was the fashion to talk of him as a sentimentalist, a romancer, a shallow wit, a nine days' wonder, a poet for "green unknowing youth."
10100-8{_}2339{_}It was a reaction, such as leads us to disestablish the heroes of our crude imaginations till we learn that to admire nothing is as sure a sign of immaturity as to admire everything.
10100-8{_}2340{_}The weariness, if not disgust, induced by a throng of more than usually absurd imitators, enabled Carlyle, the poet's successor in literary influence (followed with even greater unfairness by Thackeray), more effectively to lead the counter-revolt.
10100-8{_}2341{_}"In my mind," writes the former, in 1839, "Byron has been sinking at an accelerated rate for the last ten years, and has now reached a very low level.... His fame has been very great, but I do not see how it is to endure; neither does that make him great.
10100-8{_}2342{_}No genuine productive thought was ever revealed by him to mankind.
10100-8{_}2343{_}He taught me nothing that I had not again to forgot."
10100-8{_}2344{_}The refrain of Carlyle's advice during the most active years of his criticism was, "Close thy Byron, open thy Goethe."
10100-8{_}2345{_}We do so, and find that the refrain of Goethe's advice in reference to Byron is--"nocturn versate manu, versate diurn."
10100-8{_}2346{_}He urged Eckermann to study English that he might read him; remarking, "A character of such eminence has never existed before, and probably will never come again.
10100-8{_}2347{_}The beauty of _Cain_ is such as we shall not see a second time in the world.... Byron issues from the sea-waves ever fresh.
10100-8{_}2348{_}In _Helena_, I could not make use of any man as the representative of the modern poetic era except him, who is undoubtedly the greatest genius[1] of our century."
10100-8{_}2349{_}Again: "Tasso's epic has maintained its fame, but Byron is the burning bush, which reduces the cedar of Lebanon to ashes....
10100-8{_}2350{_}The English may think of him as they please; this is certain, they can show no (living) poet who is comparable to him....
10100-8{_}2351{_}But he is too worldly.
10100-8{_}2352{_}Contrast _Macbeth_, and _Beppo_, where you are in a nefarious empirical world."
10100-8{_}2353{_}On Eckermann's doubting "whether there is a gain for pure culture in Byron's work," Goethe conclusively replies, "There I must contradict you.
10100-8{_}2354{_}The audacity and grandeur of Byron must certainly tend towards culture.
10100-8{_}2355{_}We should take care not to be always looking for it in the decidedly pure and moral.
10100-8{_}2356{_}Everything that is great promotes cultivation, as soon as we are aware of it."
10100-8{_}2357{_}[Footnote 1: Mr. Arnold wrongly objects to this translation of the     German "talent."]
10100-8{_}2358{_}This verdict of the Olympian as against the verdict of the Titan is interesting in itself, and as being the verdict of the whole continental world of letters.
10100-8{_}2359{_}"What," exclaims Castelar, "does Spain not owe to Byron?
10100-8{_}2360{_}From his mouth come our hopes and fears.
10100-8{_}2361{_}He has baptized us with his blood.
10100-8{_}2362{_}There is no one with whose being some song of his is not woven.
10100-8{_}2363{_}His life is like a funeral torch over our graves."
10100-8{_}2364{_}Mazzini takes up the same tune for Italy.
10100-8{_}2365{_}Stendhal speaks of Byron's "Apollonic power;" and Sainte Beuve writes to the same intent, with some judicious caveats.
10100-8{_}2366{_}M. Taine concludes his survey of the romantic movement with the remark: "In this splendid effort, the greatest are exhausted.
10100-8{_}2367{_}One alone--Byron--attains the summit.
10100-8{_}2368{_}He is so great and so English, that from him alone we shall learn more truths of his country and his age than from all the rest together."
10100-8{_}2369{_}Dr. Elze, ranks the author of _Harold_ and _Juan_ among the four greatest English poets, and claims for him the intellectual parentage of Lamartine and Musset in France, of Espronceda in Spain, of Puschkin in Russia, with some modifications, of Heine in Germany, of Berchet and others in Italy.
10100-8{_}2370{_}So many voices of so various countries cannot be simply set aside: unless we wrap ourselves in an insolent insularism, we are bound at least to ask what is the meaning of their concurrent testimony.
10100-8{_}2371{_}Foreign judgments can manifestly have little weight on matters of form, and not one of the above-mentioned critics is sufficiently alive to the egregious shortcomings which Byron himself recognized.
10100-8{_}2372{_}That he loses almost nothing by translation is a compliment to the man, a disparagement to tho artist.
10100-8{_}2373{_}Very few pages of his verse even aspire to perfection; hardly a stanza will bear the minute word-by-word dissection which only brings into clearer view the delicate touches of Keats or Tennyson; his pictures with a big brush were never meant for the microscope.
10100-8{_}2374{_}Here the contrast between his theoretic worship of his idol and his own practice reaches a climax.
10100-8{_}2375{_}If, as he professed to believe, "the best poet is he who best executes his work," then he is hardly a poet at all.
10100-8{_}2376{_}He is habitually rapid and slovenly; an improvisatore on the spot whore his fancy is kindled, writing _currente calamo_, and disdaining the "art to blot."
10100-8{_}2377{_}"I can never recast anything.
10100-8{_}2378{_}I am like the tiger; if I miss the first spring, I go grumbling back to my jungle."
10100-8{_}2379{_}He said to Medwin, "Blank verse is the most difficult, because every line must be good."
10100-8{_}2380{_}Consequently, his own blank verse is always defective--sometimes execrable.
10100-8{_}2381{_}No one else--except, perhaps, Wordsworth--who could write so well, could also write so ill.
10100-8{_}2382{_}This fact in Byron's case seems due not to mere carelessness, but to incapacity.
10100-8{_}2383{_}Something seems to stand behind him, like the slave in the chariot, to check the current of his highest thought.
10100-8{_}2384{_}The glow of his fancy fades with the suddenness of a southern sunset.
10100-8{_}2385{_}His best inspirations are spoilt by the interruption of incongruous commonplace.
10100-8{_}2386{_}He had none of the guardian delicacy of taste, or the thirst after completeness, which mark the consummate artist.
10100-8{_}2387{_}He is more nearly a dwarf Shakespeare than a giant Popo.
10100-8{_}2388{_}This defect was most mischievous where he was weakest, in his dramas and his lyrics, least so where he was strongest, in his mature satires.
10100-8{_}2389{_}It is almost transmuted into an excellence in the greatest of these, which is by design and in detail a temple of incongruity.
10100-8{_}2390{_}If we turn from his manner to his matter, we cannot claim for Byron any absolute originality.
10100-8{_}2391{_}His sources have been found in Rousseau, Voltaire, Chateaubriand, Beaumarchais, Lauzun, Gibbon, Bayle, St. Pierre, Alfieri, Casti, Cuvier, La Bruyore, Wieland, Swift, Sterne, Le Sage, Goethe, scraps of the classics, and the Book of Job.
10100-8{_}2392{_}Absolute originality in a late age is only possible to the hermit, the lunatic, or the sensation novelist.
10100-8{_}2393{_}Byron, like the rovers before Minos, was not ashamed of his piracy.
10100-8{_}2394{_}He transferred the random prose of his own letters and journals to his dramas, and with the same complacency made use of the notes jotted down from other writers as he sailed on the Lake of Geneva.
10100-8{_}2395{_}But he made them his own by smelting the rough ore into bell metal.
10100-8{_}2396{_}He brewed a cauldron like that of Macbeth's witches, and from it arose the images of crowned kings.
